<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Microbiology">Microbiology</span></h3>
<p>In people managed in the community, determining the causative agent is not cost-effective and typically does not alter management.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Develop11_24-6"><a href="#cite_note-Develop11-24">[23]</a></sup> For people who do not respond to treatment, <a href="/wiki/Sputum_culture" title="Sputum culture">sputum culture</a> should be considered, and culture for <i><a href="/wiki/Mycobacterium_tuberculosis" title="Mycobacterium tuberculosis">Mycobacterium tuberculosis</a></i> should be carried out in persons with a chronic productive cough.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BTS09_63-4"><a href="#cite_note-BTS09-63">[62]</a></sup> Microbiological evaluation is also indicated in severe pneumonia, alcoholism, <a href="/wiki/Asplenia" title="Asplenia">asplenia</a>, immunosuppression, HIV infection, and those being empirically treated for MRSA of pseudomonas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elena_2015_36-8"><a href="#cite_note-Elena_2015-36">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Met2019_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Met2019-72">[71]</a></sup> Although positive <a href="/wiki/Blood_culture" title="Blood culture">blood culture</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pleural_fluid" title="Pleural fluid">pleural fluid</a> culture definitively establish the diagnosis of the type of micro-organism involved, a positive sputum culture has to be interpreted with care for the possibility of <a href="/wiki/Colonisation_(biology)" title="Colonisation (biology)">colonisation</a> of respiratory tract.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elena_2015_36-9"><a href="#cite_note-Elena_2015-36">[35]</a></sup> Testing for other specific organisms may be recommended during outbreaks, for public health reasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BTS09_63-5"><a href="#cite_note-BTS09-63">[62]</a></sup> In those hospitalized for severe disease, both sputum and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blood_cultures" title="Blood cultures">blood cultures</a> are recommended,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BTS09_63-6"><a href="#cite_note-BTS09-63">[62]</a></sup> as well as testing the urine for <a href="/wiki/Antigen" title="Antigen">antigens</a> to <i>Legionella</i> and <i>Streptococcus</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IDSA2007_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-IDSA2007-73">[72]</a></sup> Viral infections, can be confirmed via detection of either the virus or its antigens with <a href="/wiki/Viral_culture" title="Viral culture">culture</a> or <a href="/wiki/Polymerase_chain_reaction" title="Polymerase chain reaction">polymerase chain reaction</a> (PCR), among other techniques.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet11_12-10"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet11-12">[12]</a></sup> <i>Mycoplasma</i>, <i>Legionella</i>, <i>Streptococcus</i>, and <i>Chlamydia</i> can also be detected using PCR techniques on <a href="/wiki/Bronchoalveolar_lavage" title="Bronchoalveolar lavage">bronchoalveolar lavage</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nasopharyngeal_swab" title="Nasopharyngeal swab">nasopharyngeal swab</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elena_2015_36-10"><a href="#cite_note-Elena_2015-36">[35]</a></sup> The causative agent is determined in only 15% of cases with routine microbiological tests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BMJ06_9-4"><a href="#cite_note-BMJ06-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Classification_of_pneumonia" title="Classification of pneumonia">Classification of pneumonia</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pneumonitis" title="Pneumonitis">Pneumonitis</a> refers to lung <a href="/wiki/Inflammation" title="Inflammation">inflammation</a>; pneumonia refers to pneumonitis, usually due to infection but sometimes non-infectious, that has the additional feature of <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_consolidation" title="Pulmonary consolidation">pulmonary consolidation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup> Pneumonia is most commonly classified by where or how it was acquired: <a href="/wiki/Community-acquired_pneumonia" title="Community-acquired pneumonia">community-acquired</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aspiration_pneumonia" title="Aspiration pneumonia">aspiration</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Healthcare-associated_pneumonia" title="Healthcare-associated pneumonia">healthcare-associated</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hospital-acquired_pneumonia" title="Hospital-acquired pneumonia">hospital-acquired</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ventilator-associated_pneumonia" title="Ventilator-associated pneumonia">ventilator-associated pneumonia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rad07_37-7"><a href="#cite_note-Rad07-37">[36]</a></sup> It may also be classified by the area of the lung affected: <a href="/wiki/Lobar_pneumonia" title="Lobar pneumonia">lobar pneumonia</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bronchial_pneumonia" title="Bronchial pneumonia">bronchial pneumonia</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acute_interstitial_pneumonia" title="Acute interstitial pneumonia">acute interstitial pneumonia</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rad07_37-8"><a href="#cite_note-Rad07-37">[36]</a></sup> or by the causative organism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup> Pneumonia in children may additionally be classified based on signs and symptoms as non-severe, severe, or very severe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>The setting in which pneumonia develops is important to treatment,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ana2009_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ana2009-77">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ATS2005_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-ATS2005-78">[77]</a></sup> as it correlates to which pathogens are likely suspects,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ana2009_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ana2009-77">[76]</a></sup> which mechanisms are likely, which antibiotics are likely to work or fail,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ana2009_77-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ana2009-77">[76]</a></sup> and which complications can be expected based on the person's health status.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Community">Community</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Community-acquired_pneumonia" title="Community-acquired pneumonia">Community-acquired pneumonia</a></div>
<p>Community-acquired pneumonia (CAP) is acquired in the community,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ana2009_77-3"><a href="#cite_note-Ana2009-77">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ATS2005_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-ATS2005-78">[77]</a></sup> outside of health care facilities. Compared with healthcare-associated pneumonia, it is less likely to involve <a href="/wiki/Multiple_drug_resistance" title="Multiple drug resistance">multidrug-resistant</a> bacteria. Although the latter are no longer rare in CAP,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ana2009_77-4"><a href="#cite_note-Ana2009-77">[76]</a></sup> they are still less likely.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Healthcare">Healthcare</span></h4>
<p>Health care–associated pneumonia (HCAP) is an infection associated with recent exposure to the <a href="/wiki/Health_care" title="Health care">health care</a> system,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ana2009_77-5"><a href="#cite_note-Ana2009-77">[76]</a></sup> including hospitals, outpatient clinics, <a href="/wiki/Nursing_home" title="Nursing home">nursing homes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kidney_dialysis" title="Kidney dialysis">dialysis</a> centers, <a href="/wiki/Chemotherapy" title="Chemotherapy">chemotherapy</a> treatment, or <a href="/wiki/Home_care" title="Home care">home care</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ATS2005_78-2"><a href="#cite_note-ATS2005-78">[77]</a></sup> HCAP is sometimes called MCAP (medical care–associated pneumonia).
</p><p>People may become infected with pneumonia in a hospital; this is defined as pneumonia not present at the time of admission (symptoms must start at least 48 hours after admission).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ATS2005_78-3"><a href="#cite_note-ATS2005-78">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ana2009_77-6"><a href="#cite_note-Ana2009-77">[76]</a></sup> It is likely to involve <a href="/wiki/Hospital-acquired_infection" title="Hospital-acquired infection">hospital-acquired infections</a>, with higher risk of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multidrug-resistant" title="Multidrug-resistant">multidrug-resistant</a> pathogens. People in a hospital often have other medical conditions, which may make them more susceptible to pathogens in the hospital.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ventilator-associated_pneumonia" title="Ventilator-associated pneumonia">Ventilator-associated pneumonia</a> occurs in people breathing with the help of <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_ventilation" title="Mechanical ventilation">mechanical ventilation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ana2009_77-7"><a href="#cite_note-Ana2009-77">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ar2016_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ar2016-34">[33]</a></sup> Ventilator-associated pneumonia is specifically defined as pneumonia that arises more than 48 to 72 hours after <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endotracheal_intubation" title="Endotracheal intubation">endotracheal intubation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ATS2005_78-4"><a href="#cite_note-ATS2005-78">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Differential_diagnosis">Differential diagnosis</span></h3>
<p>Several diseases can present with similar signs and symptoms to pneumonia, such as: <a href="/wiki/Chronic_obstructive_pulmonary_disease" title="Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease">chronic obstructive pulmonary disease</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asthma" title="Asthma">asthma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_edema" title="Pulmonary edema">pulmonary edema</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bronchiectasis" title="Bronchiectasis">bronchiectasis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lung_cancer" title="Lung cancer">lung cancer</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pulmonary_emboli" title="Pulmonary emboli">pulmonary emboli</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BMJ06_9-5"><a href="#cite_note-BMJ06-9">[9]</a></sup> Unlike pneumonia, asthma and COPD typically present with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wheezing" title="Wheezing">wheezing</a>, pulmonary edema presents with an abnormal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrocardiogram" title="Electrocardiogram">electrocardiogram</a>, cancer and bronchiectasis present with a cough of longer duration, and pulmonary emboli present with acute onset sharp chest pain and <a href="/wiki/Shortness_of_breath" title="Shortness of breath">shortness of breath</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BMJ06_9-6"><a href="#cite_note-BMJ06-9">[9]</a></sup> Mild pneumonia should be differentiated from <a href="/wiki/Upper_respiratory_tract_infection" title="Upper respiratory tract infection">upper respiratory tract infection</a> (URTI). Severe pneumonia should be differentiated from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acute_heart_failure" title="Acute heart failure">acute heart failure</a>. Pulmonary infiltrates that resolved after giving <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_ventilation" title="Mechanical ventilation">mechanical ventilation</a> should point to heart failure and <a href="/wiki/Atelectasis" title="Atelectasis">atelectasis</a> rather than pneumonia. For recurrent pneumonia, underlying lung cancer, <a href="/wiki/Metastasis" title="Metastasis">metastasis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a>, a foreign bodies, immunosuppression, and hypersensitivity should be suspected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elena_2015_36-11"><a href="#cite_note-Elena_2015-36">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prevention">Prevention</span></h2>
<p>Prevention includes <a href="/wiki/Vaccination" title="Vaccination">vaccination</a>, environmental measures, and appropriate treatment of other health problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Develop11_24-7"><a href="#cite_note-Develop11-24">[23]</a></sup> It is believed that, if appropriate preventive measures were instituted globally, mortality among children could be reduced by 400,000; and, if proper treatment were universally available, childhood deaths could be decreased by another 600,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOPrevent2012_26-3"><a href="#cite_note-WHOPrevent2012-26">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vaccination">Vaccination</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Vaccination" title="Vaccination">Vaccination</a> prevents against certain bacterial and viral pneumonias both in children and adults. <a href="/wiki/Influenza_vaccine" title="Influenza vaccine">Influenza vaccines</a> are modestly effective at preventing symptoms of influenza,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet11_12-11"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet11-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Center_for_Disease_Control_and_Prevention" title="Center for Disease Control and Prevention">Center for Disease Control and Prevention</a> (CDC) recommends yearly influenza vaccination for every person 6 months and older.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup> Immunizing health care workers decreases the risk of viral pneumonia among their patients.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IDSA2007_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-IDSA2007-73">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>Vaccinations against <i><a href="/wiki/Haemophilus_influenzae" title="Haemophilus influenzae">Haemophilus influenzae</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Streptococcus_pneumoniae" title="Streptococcus pneumoniae">Streptococcus pneumoniae</a></i> have good evidence to support their use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PedNA09_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-PedNA09-50">[49]</a></sup> There is strong evidence for vaccinating children under the age of 2 against <i>Streptococcus pneumoniae</i> (<a href="/wiki/Pneumococcal_conjugate_vaccine" title="Pneumococcal conjugate vaccine">pneumococcal conjugate vaccine</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2018Vac_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2018Vac-83">[82]</a></sup> Vaccinating children against <i>Streptococcus pneumoniae</i> has led to a decreased rate of these infections in adults, because many adults acquire infections from children. A <a href="/wiki/Pneumococcal_polysaccharide_vaccine" title="Pneumococcal polysaccharide vaccine"><i>Streptococcus pneumoniae</i> vaccine</a> is available for adults, and has been found to decrease the risk of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Invasive_pneumococcal_disease" title="Invasive pneumococcal disease">invasive pneumococcal disease</a> by 74%, but there is insufficient evidence to suggest using the pneumococcal vaccine to prevent pneumonia or death in the general adult population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup> The CDC recommends that young children and adults over the age of 65 receive the pneumococcal vaccine, as well as older children or younger adults who have an increased risk of getting pneumococcal disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2018Vac_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2018Vac-83">[82]</a></sup> The pneumococcal vaccine has been shown to reduce the risk of community acquired pneumonia in people with <a href="/wiki/Chronic_obstructive_pulmonary_disease" title="Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease">chronic obstructive pulmonary disease</a>, but does not reduce mortality or the risk of hospitalization for people with this condition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walters2017_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Walters2017-85">[84]</a></sup> People with COPD are recommended by a number of guidelines to have a pneumococcal vaccination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walters2017_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-Walters2017-85">[84]</a></sup> Other vaccines for which there is support for a protective effect against pneumonia include <a href="/wiki/Pertussis_vaccine" title="Pertussis vaccine">pertussis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Varicella_vaccine" title="Varicella vaccine">varicella</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Measles_vaccine" title="Measles vaccine">measles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDCPrev2012_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDCPrev2012-86">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medications">Medications</span></h3>
<p>When influenza outbreaks occur, medications such as <a href="/wiki/Amantadine" title="Amantadine">amantadine</a> or <a href="/wiki/Rimantadine" title="Rimantadine">rimantadine</a> may help prevent the condition, but they are associated with side effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jefferson_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-jefferson-87">[86]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Zanamivir" title="Zanamivir">Zanamivir</a> or <a href="/wiki/Oseltamivir" title="Oseltamivir">oseltamivir</a> decrease the chance that people who are exposed to the virus will develop symptoms; however, it is recommended that potential side effects are taken into account.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other">Other</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Smoking_cessation" title="Smoking cessation">Smoking cessation</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BTS09_63-7"><a href="#cite_note-BTS09-63">[62]</a></sup> and reducing <a href="/wiki/Indoor_air_quality" title="Indoor air quality">indoor air pollution</a>, such as that from cooking indoors with wood, crop residues or <a href="/wiki/Feces" title="Feces">dung</a>, are both recommended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Develop11_24-8"><a href="#cite_note-Develop11-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOPrevent2012_26-4"><a href="#cite_note-WHOPrevent2012-26">[25]</a></sup> Smoking appears to be the single biggest risk factor for <a href="/wiki/Pneumococcal_pneumonia" title="Pneumococcal pneumonia">pneumococcal pneumonia</a> in otherwise-healthy adults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IDSA2007_73-2"><a href="#cite_note-IDSA2007-73">[72]</a></sup> Hand hygiene and coughing into one's sleeve may also be effective preventative measures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDCPrev2012_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-CDCPrev2012-86">[85]</a></sup> Wearing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surgical_masks" title="Surgical masks">surgical masks</a> by the sick may also prevent illness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IDSA2007_73-3"><a href="#cite_note-IDSA2007-73">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>Appropriately treating underlying illnesses (such as <a href="/wiki/HIV/AIDS" title="HIV/AIDS">HIV/AIDS</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diabetes_mellitus" title="Diabetes mellitus">diabetes mellitus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">malnutrition</a>) can decrease the risk of pneumonia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOPrevent2012_26-5"><a href="#cite_note-WHOPrevent2012-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDCPrev2012_86-2"><a href="#cite_note-CDCPrev2012-86">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[88]</a></sup> In children less than 6 months of age, exclusive breast feeding reduces both the risk and severity of disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOPrevent2012_26-6"><a href="#cite_note-WHOPrevent2012-26">[25]</a></sup> In people with HIV/AIDS and a CD4 count of less than 200 cells/uL the antibiotic <a href="/wiki/Trimethoprim/sulfamethoxazole" title="Trimethoprim/sulfamethoxazole">trimethoprim/sulfamethoxazole</a> decreases the risk of <i><a href="/wiki/Pneumocystis_pneumonia" title="Pneumocystis pneumonia">Pneumocystis pneumonia</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[89]</a></sup> and is also useful for prevention in those that are immunocompromised but do not have HIV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>Testing pregnant women for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Group_B_Streptococcus" title="Group B Streptococcus">Group B Streptococcus</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Chlamydia_trachomatis" title="Chlamydia trachomatis">Chlamydia trachomatis</a></i>, and administering <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic" title="Antibiotic">antibiotic</a> treatment, if needed, reduces rates of pneumonia in infants;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[92]</a></sup> preventive measures for HIV transmission from mother to child may also be efficient.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[93]</a></sup> Suctioning the mouth and throat of infants with <a href="/wiki/Meconium" title="Meconium">meconium</a>-stained <a href="/wiki/Amniotic_fluid" title="Amniotic fluid">amniotic fluid</a> has not been found to reduce the rate of <a href="/wiki/Aspiration_pneumonia" title="Aspiration pneumonia">aspiration pneumonia</a> and may cause potential harm,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rog2009_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rog2009-95">[94]</a></sup> thus this practice is not recommended in the majority of situations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rog2009_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rog2009-95">[94]</a></sup> In the frail elderly good oral health care may lower the risk of aspiration pneumonia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zinc_supplementation" title="Zinc supplementation">Zinc supplementation</a> in children 2 months to five years old appears to reduce rates of pneumonia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>For people with low levels of <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_C" title="Vitamin C">vitamin C</a> in their diet or blood, taking vitamin C supplements may be suggested to decrease the risk of pneumonia, although there is no strong evidence of benefit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-98">[97]</a></sup> There is insufficient evidence to recommend that the general population take vitamin C to prevent or treat pneumonia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-98">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>For adults and children in the hospital who require a respirator, there is no strong evidence indicating a difference between <a href="/wiki/Heat_and_moisture_exchanger" title="Heat and moisture exchanger">heat and moisture exchangers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Humidifier" title="Humidifier">heated humidifiers</a> for preventing pneumonia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[98]</a></sup> There is no good evidence that one approach to mouth care is better than others in preventing nursing home acquired pneumonia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[99]</a></sup> There is tentative evidence that laying flat on the back compared to semi-raised increases pneumonia risks in people who are intubated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Management">Management</span></h2>
<table align="right" class="wikitable" style="margin-left:0.4em;">
<tbody><tr>
<th colspan="2"><a href="/wiki/CURB-65" title="CURB-65">CURB-65</a>
</th></tr>
<tr>
<th>Symptom</th>
<th>Points
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>C</b>onfusion</td>
<td style="text-align: center;">1
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>U</b>rea&gt;7 mmol/L</td>
<td style="text-align: center;">1
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>R</b>espiratory rate&gt;30</td>
<td style="text-align: center;">1
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Blood_pressure" title="Blood pressure">S<b>B</b>P</a>&lt;90mmHg, D<b>B</b>P&lt;60mmHg</td>
<td style="text-align: center;">1
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Age&gt;=<b>65</b></td>
<td style="text-align: center;">1
</td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibiotics" title="Antibiotics">Antibiotics</a> by mouth, rest, simple <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Analgesics" title="Analgesics">analgesics</a>, and fluids usually suffice for complete resolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BTS09_63-8"><a href="#cite_note-BTS09-63">[62]</a></sup> However, those with other medical conditions, the elderly, or those with significant trouble breathing may require more advanced care. If the symptoms worsen, the pneumonia does not improve with home treatment, or complications occur, hospitalization may be required.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BTS09_63-9"><a href="#cite_note-BTS09-63">[62]</a></sup> Worldwide, approximately 7–13% of cases in children result in hospitalization,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Develop11_24-9"><a href="#cite_note-Develop11-24">[23]</a></sup> whereas in the developed world between 22 and 42% of adults with community-acquired pneumonia are admitted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BTS09_63-10"><a href="#cite_note-BTS09-63">[62]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/CURB-65" title="CURB-65">CURB-65</a> score is useful for determining the need for admission in adults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BTS09_63-11"><a href="#cite_note-BTS09-63">[62]</a></sup> If the score is 0 or 1, people can typically be managed at home; if it is 2, a short hospital stay or close follow-up is needed; if it is 3–5, hospitalization is recommended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BTS09_63-12"><a href="#cite_note-BTS09-63">[62]</a></sup> In children those with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dyspnea" title="Dyspnea">respiratory distress</a> or oxygen saturations of less than 90% should be hospitalized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PIDS11_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-PIDS11-102">[101]</a></sup> The utility of <a href="/wiki/Chest_physiotherapy" title="Chest physiotherapy">chest physiotherapy</a> in pneumonia has not yet been determined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[103]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="Updated version https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/36066373 (October 2022)">needs update</span></a></i>]</sup> Over-the-counter <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cough_medicine" title="Cough medicine">cough medicine</a> has not been found to be effective,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chang2014_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chang2014-105">[104]</a></sup> nor has the use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zinc_supplementation" title="Zinc supplementation">zinc</a> in children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[105]</a></sup> There is insufficient evidence for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mucolytics" title="Mucolytics">mucolytics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chang2014_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chang2014-105">[104]</a></sup> There is no strong evidence to recommend that children who have non-measles related pneumonia take <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_A" title="Vitamin A">vitamin A</a> supplements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[106]</a></sup> Vitamin D, as of 2018 is of unclear benefit in children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p>Pneumonia can cause severe illness in a number of ways, and pneumonia with evidence of organ dysfunction may require <a href="/wiki/Intensive_care_unit" title="Intensive care unit">intensive care unit</a> admission for observation and specific treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phua_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Phua-109">[108]</a></sup> The main impact is on the respiratory and the circulatory system. <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_failure" title="Respiratory failure">Respiratory failure</a> not responding to normal oxygen therapy may require <a href="/wiki/Heated_humidified_high-flow_therapy" title="Heated humidified high-flow therapy">heated humidified high-flow therapy</a> delivered through nasal cannulae,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phua_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-Phua-109">[108]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Non-invasive_ventilation" title="Non-invasive ventilation">non-invasive ventilation</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[109]</a></sup> or in severe cases <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_ventilation" title="Mechanical ventilation">invasive ventilation</a> through an endotracheal tube.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phua_109-2"><a href="#cite_note-Phua-109">[108]</a></sup> Regarding circulatory problems as part of <a href="/wiki/Sepsis" title="Sepsis">sepsis</a>, evidence of poor blood flow or low blood pressure is initially treated with 30 mL/kg of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crystalloid_solution" title="Crystalloid solution">crystalloid</a> infused intravenously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elena_2015_36-12"><a href="#cite_note-Elena_2015-36">[35]</a></sup> In situations where fluids alone are ineffective, <a href="/wiki/Antihypotensive_agent" title="Antihypotensive agent">vasopressor</a> medication may be required.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phua_109-3"><a href="#cite_note-Phua-109">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>For adults with moderate or severe <a href="/wiki/Acute_respiratory_distress_syndrome" title="Acute respiratory distress syndrome">acute respiratory distress syndrome</a> (ARDS) undergoing mechanical ventilation, there is a reduction in mortality when people <a href="/wiki/Proning" title="Proning">lay on their front</a> for at least 12 hours a day. However, this increases the risk of endotracheal tube obstruction and pressure sores.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[110]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bacterial_2">Bacterial</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Antibiotic" title="Antibiotic">Antibiotics</a> improve outcomes in those with bacterial pneumonia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CochraneTx13_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-CochraneTx13-13">[13]</a></sup> The first dose of antibiotics should be given as soon as possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elena_2015_36-13"><a href="#cite_note-Elena_2015-36">[35]</a></sup> Increased use of antibiotics, however, may lead to the development of <a href="/wiki/Antimicrobial_resistance" title="Antimicrobial resistance">antimicrobial resistant</a> strains of bacteria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pak2014_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pak2014-112">[111]</a></sup> Antibiotic choice depends initially on the characteristics of the person affected, such as age, underlying health, and the location the infection was acquired. Antibiotic use is also associated with side effects such as nausea, diarrhea, dizziness, taste distortion, or headaches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pak2014_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pak2014-112">[111]</a></sup> In the UK, <a href="/wiki/Empiric_therapy" title="Empiric therapy">treatment before culture results</a> with <a href="/wiki/Amoxicillin" title="Amoxicillin">amoxicillin</a> is recommended as the first line for <a href="/wiki/Community-acquired_pneumonia" title="Community-acquired pneumonia">community-acquired pneumonia</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Doxycycline" title="Doxycycline">doxycycline</a> or <a href="/wiki/Clarithromycin" title="Clarithromycin">clarithromycin</a> as alternatives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BTS09_63-13"><a href="#cite_note-BTS09-63">[62]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>, amoxicillin, doxycycline, and in some areas a <a href="/wiki/Macrolide" title="Macrolide">macrolide</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Azithromycin" title="Azithromycin">azithromycin</a> or <a href="/wiki/Erythromycin" title="Erythromycin">erythromycin</a>) is the first-line outpatient treatment in adults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EOP10_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-EOP10-38">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lutfiyya_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lutfiyya-113">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Met2019_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-Met2019-72">[71]</a></sup> In children with mild or moderate symptoms, amoxicillin taken by mouth is the first line.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PIDS11_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-PIDS11-102">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup> The use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fluoroquinolones" title="Fluoroquinolones">fluoroquinolones</a> in uncomplicated cases is discouraged due to concerns about side-effects and generating resistance in light of there being no greater benefit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EOP10_38-2"><a href="#cite_note-EOP10-38">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>For those who require hospitalization and caught their pneumonia in the community the use of a β-lactam such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cephazolin" title="Cephazolin">cephazolin</a> plus <a href="/wiki/Macrolide" title="Macrolide">macrolide</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Azithromycin" title="Azithromycin">azithromycin</a> is recommended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Met2019_72-2"><a href="#cite_note-Met2019-72">[71]</a></sup> A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fluoroquinolone" title="Fluoroquinolone">fluoroquinolone</a> may replace azithromycin but is less preferred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Met2019_72-3"><a href="#cite_note-Met2019-72">[71]</a></sup> Antibiotics by mouth and by injection appear to be similarly effective in children with severe pneumonia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roj2006_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roj2006-118">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>The duration of treatment has traditionally been seven to ten days, but increasing evidence suggests that shorter courses (3–5 days) may be effective for certain types of pneumonia and may reduce the risk of antibiotic resistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pug2015_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pug2015-121">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[121]</a></sup> Research in children showed that a shorter, 3-day course of amoxicillin was as effective as a longer, 7-day course for treating pneumonia in this population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[123]</a></sup> For pneumonia that is associated with a ventilator caused by non-fermenting Gram-negative bacilli (NF-GNB), a shorter course of antibiotics increases the risk that the pneumonia will return.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pug2015_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pug2015-121">[120]</a></sup> Recommendations for <a href="/wiki/Hospital-acquired_pneumonia" title="Hospital-acquired pneumonia">hospital-acquired pneumonia</a> include third- and fourth-generation <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cephalosporins" title="Cephalosporins">cephalosporins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbapenem" title="Carbapenem">carbapenems</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fluoroquinolone" title="Fluoroquinolone">fluoroquinolones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aminoglycoside" title="Aminoglycoside">aminoglycosides</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Vancomycin" title="Vancomycin">vancomycin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ATS2005_78-5"><a href="#cite_note-ATS2005-78">[77]</a></sup> These antibiotics are often given <a href="/wiki/Intravenous_therapy" title="Intravenous therapy">intravenously</a> and used in combination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ATS2005_78-6"><a href="#cite_note-ATS2005-78">[77]</a></sup> In those treated in hospital, more than 90% improve with the initial antibiotics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M32_29-5"><a href="#cite_note-M32-29">[28]</a></sup> For people with ventilator-acquired pneumonia, the choice of antibiotic therapy will depend on the person's risk of being infected with a strain of bacteria that is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multi-drug_resistant_bacteria" title="Multi-drug resistant bacteria">multi-drug resistant</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ar2016_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ar2016-34">[33]</a></sup> Once clinically stable, intravenous antibiotics should be switched to oral antibiotics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elena_2015_36-14"><a href="#cite_note-Elena_2015-36">[35]</a></sup> For those with <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methicillin_resistant_Staphylococcus_aureus" title="Methicillin resistant Staphylococcus aureus">Methicillin resistant Staphylococcus aureus</a></i> (MRSA) or <i>Legionella</i> infections, prolonged antibiotics may be beneficial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elena_2015_36-15"><a href="#cite_note-Elena_2015-36">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>The addition of <a href="/wiki/Corticosteroid" title="Corticosteroid">corticosteroids</a> to standard antibiotic treatment appears to improve outcomes, reducing death and morbidity for adults with severe community acquired pneumonia, and reducing death for adults and children with non-severe community acquired pneumonia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stern2017_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stern2017-125">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[125]</a></sup> A 2017 review therefore recommended them in adults with severe community acquired pneumonia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stern2017_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stern2017-125">[124]</a></sup> A 2019 guideline however recommended against their general use, unless refractory shock was present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Met2019_72-4"><a href="#cite_note-Met2019-72">[71]</a></sup> Side effects associated with the use of corticosteroids include high blood sugar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stern2017_125-2"><a href="#cite_note-Stern2017-125">[124]</a></sup> There is some evidence that adding corticosteroids to the standard PCP pneumonia treatment may be beneficial for people who are infected with HIV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ewa2015_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ewa2015-44">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>The use of granulocyte colony stimulating factor (G-CSF) along with antibiotics does not appear to reduce mortality and routine use for treating pneumonia is not supported by evidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[126]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Viral_2">Viral</span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neuraminidase_inhibitors" title="Neuraminidase inhibitors">Neuraminidase inhibitors</a> may be used to treat <a href="/wiki/Viral_pneumonia" title="Viral pneumonia">viral pneumonia</a> caused by influenza viruses (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Influenza_A" title="Influenza A">influenza A</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Influenza_B" title="Influenza B">influenza B</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet11_12-12"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet11-12">[12]</a></sup> No specific <a href="/wiki/Antiviral_drug" title="Antiviral drug">antiviral</a> medications are recommended for other types of community acquired viral pneumonias including <a href="/wiki/SARS" title="SARS">SARS coronavirus</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adenovirus" title="Adenovirus">adenovirus</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hantavirus" title="Hantavirus">hantavirus</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parainfluenza" title="Parainfluenza">parainfluenza</a> virus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet11_12-13"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet11-12">[12]</a></sup> Influenza A may be treated with <a href="/wiki/Rimantadine" title="Rimantadine">rimantadine</a> or <a href="/wiki/Amantadine" title="Amantadine">amantadine</a>, while influenza A or B may be treated with <a href="/wiki/Oseltamivir" title="Oseltamivir">oseltamivir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zanamivir" title="Zanamivir">zanamivir</a> or <a href="/wiki/Peramivir" title="Peramivir">peramivir</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet11_12-14"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet11-12">[12]</a></sup> These are of most benefit if they are started within 48 hours of the onset of symptoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet11_12-15"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet11-12">[12]</a></sup> Many strains of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/H5N1" title="H5N1">H5N1</a> influenza A, also known as <a href="/wiki/Avian_influenza" title="Avian influenza">avian influenza</a> or "bird flu", have shown resistance to rimantadine and amantadine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet11_12-16"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet11-12">[12]</a></sup> The use of antibiotics in viral pneumonia is recommended by some experts, as it is impossible to rule out a complicating bacterial infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet11_12-17"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet11-12">[12]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/British_Thoracic_Society" title="British Thoracic Society">British Thoracic Society</a> recommends that antibiotics be withheld in those with mild disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet11_12-18"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet11-12">[12]</a></sup> The use of <a href="/wiki/Corticosteroid" title="Corticosteroid">corticosteroids</a> is controversial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet11_12-19"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet11-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aspiration">Aspiration</span></h3>
<p>In general, <a href="/wiki/Chemical_pneumonitis" title="Chemical pneumonitis">aspiration pneumonitis</a> is treated conservatively with antibiotics indicated only for <a href="/wiki/Aspiration_pneumonia" title="Aspiration pneumonia">aspiration pneumonia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PA2011_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-PA2011-128">[127]</a></sup> The choice of antibiotic will depend on several factors, including the suspected causative organism and whether pneumonia was acquired in the community or developed in a hospital setting. Common options include <a href="/wiki/Clindamycin" title="Clindamycin">clindamycin</a>, a combination of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beta-lactam_antibiotic" title="Beta-lactam antibiotic">beta-lactam antibiotic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Metronidazole" title="Metronidazole">metronidazole</a>, or an <a href="/wiki/Aminoglycoside" title="Aminoglycoside">aminoglycoside</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OConnor_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-OConnor-129">[128]</a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/Corticosteroid" title="Corticosteroid">Corticosteroids</a> are sometimes used in aspiration pneumonia, but there is limited evidence to support their effectiveness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PA2011_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-PA2011-128">[127]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Follow-up">Follow-up</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/British_Thoracic_Society" title="British Thoracic Society">British Thoracic Society</a> recommends that a follow-up chest radiograph be taken in people with persistent symptoms, smokers, and people older than 50.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BTS09_63-14"><a href="#cite_note-BTS09-63">[62]</a></sup> American guidelines vary, from generally recommending a follow-up chest radiograph<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[129]</a></sup> to not mentioning any follow-up.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IDSA2007_73-4"><a href="#cite_note-IDSA2007-73">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prognosis">Prognosis</span></h2>
<p>With treatment, most types of bacterial pneumonia will stabilize in 3–6 days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Behera2010_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Behera2010-2">[2]</a></sup> It often takes a few weeks before most symptoms resolve.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Behera2010_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Behera2010-2">[2]</a></sup> X-ray findings typically clear within four weeks and mortality is low (less than 1%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clinic2011_25-15"><a href="#cite_note-Clinic2011-25">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-C6_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-C6-131">[130]</a></sup> In the elderly or people with other lung problems, recovery may take more than 12 weeks. In persons requiring hospitalization, mortality may be as high as 10%, and in those requiring intensive care it may reach 30–50%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clinic2011_25-16"><a href="#cite_note-Clinic2011-25">[24]</a></sup> Pneumonia is the most common <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nosocomial_infection" title="Nosocomial infection">hospital-acquired infection</a> that causes death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M32_29-6"><a href="#cite_note-M32-29">[28]</a></sup> Before the advent of antibiotics, mortality was typically 30% in those that were hospitalized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBMED05_22-8"><a href="#cite_note-EBMED05-22">[21]</a></sup> However, for those whose lung condition deteriorates within 72 hours, the problem is usually due to sepsis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elena_2015_36-16"><a href="#cite_note-Elena_2015-36">[35]</a></sup> If pneumonia deteriorates after 72 hours, it could be due to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nosocomial_infection" title="Nosocomial infection">nosocomial infection</a> or excerbation of other underlying comorbidities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elena_2015_36-17"><a href="#cite_note-Elena_2015-36">[35]</a></sup> About 10% of those discharged from hospital are readmitted due to underlying co-morbidities such as heart, lung, or neurological disorders, or due to new onset of pneumonia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elena_2015_36-18"><a href="#cite_note-Elena_2015-36">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>Complications may occur in particular in the elderly and those with underlying health problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-C6_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-C6-131">[130]</a></sup> This may include, among others: <a href="/wiki/Empyema" title="Empyema">empyema</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lung_abscess" title="Lung abscess">lung abscess</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bronchiolitis_obliterans" title="Bronchiolitis obliterans">bronchiolitis obliterans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acute_respiratory_distress_syndrome" title="Acute respiratory distress syndrome">acute respiratory distress syndrome</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sepsis" title="Sepsis">sepsis</a>, and worsening of underlying health problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-C6_131-2"><a href="#cite_note-C6-131">[130]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Clinical_prediction_rules">Clinical prediction rules</span></h3>
<p>Clinical prediction rules have been developed to more objectively predict outcomes of pneumonia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M32_29-7"><a href="#cite_note-M32-29">[28]</a></sup> These rules are often used to decide whether to hospitalize the person.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M32_29-8"><a href="#cite_note-M32-29">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Pneumonia_severity_index" title="Pneumonia severity index">Pneumonia severity index</a> (or <i>PSI Score</i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M32_29-9"><a href="#cite_note-M32-29">[28]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/CURB-65" title="CURB-65">CURB-65</a> score, which takes into account the severity of symptoms, any underlying diseases, and age<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[131]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span id="Pleural_effusion.2C_empyema.2C_and_abscess"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Pleural_effusion,_empyema,_and_abscess">Pleural effusion, empyema, and abscess</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pleural_effusion.jpg"><img alt="An X-ray showing a chest lying horizontally. The lower black area, which is the right lung, is smaller with a whiter area below it of a pulmonary effusion. Red arrows indicate size." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="382" data-file-width="475" decoding="async" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Pleural_effusion.jpg/220px-Pleural_effusion.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Pleural_effusion.jpg/330px-Pleural_effusion.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Pleural_effusion.jpg/440px-Pleural_effusion.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pleural_effusion.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Pleural_effusion" title="Pleural effusion">pleural effusion</a>: as seen on chest X-ray. The A arrow indicates fluid layering in the right chest. The B arrow indicates the width of the right lung. The volume of the lung is reduced because of the collection of fluid around the lung.</div></div></div>
<p>In pneumonia, a <a href="/wiki/Pleural_effusion" title="Pleural effusion">collection of fluid</a> may form in the <a href="/wiki/Pleural_cavity" title="Pleural cavity">space that surrounds the lung</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yu2011_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yu2011-133">[132]</a></sup> Occasionally, microorganisms will infect this fluid, causing an <a href="/wiki/Pleural_empyema" title="Pleural empyema">empyema</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yu2011_133-1"><a href="#cite_note-Yu2011-133">[132]</a></sup> To distinguish an empyema from the more common simple <a href="/wiki/Parapneumonic_effusion" title="Parapneumonic effusion">parapneumonic effusion</a>, the fluid may be collected with a needle (<a href="/wiki/Thoracentesis" title="Thoracentesis">thoracentesis</a>), and examined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yu2011_133-2"><a href="#cite_note-Yu2011-133">[132]</a></sup> If this shows evidence of empyema, complete drainage of the fluid is necessary, often requiring a <a href="/wiki/Chest_tube" title="Chest tube">drainage catheter</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yu2011_133-3"><a href="#cite_note-Yu2011-133">[132]</a></sup> In severe cases of empyema, <a href="/wiki/Decortication" title="Decortication">surgery</a> may be needed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yu2011_133-4"><a href="#cite_note-Yu2011-133">[132]</a></sup> If the infected fluid is not drained, the infection may persist, because antibiotics do not penetrate well into the pleural cavity. If the fluid is sterile, it must be drained only if it is causing symptoms or remains unresolved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yu2011_133-5"><a href="#cite_note-Yu2011-133">[132]</a></sup>
</p><p>In rare circumstances, bacteria in the lung will form a pocket of infected fluid called a <a href="/wiki/Lung_abscess" title="Lung abscess">lung abscess</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yu2011_133-6"><a href="#cite_note-Yu2011-133">[132]</a></sup> Lung abscesses can usually be seen with a chest X-ray but frequently require a chest CT scan to confirm the diagnosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yu2011_133-7"><a href="#cite_note-Yu2011-133">[132]</a></sup> Abscesses typically occur in <a href="/wiki/Aspiration_pneumonia" title="Aspiration pneumonia">aspiration pneumonia</a>, and often contain several types of bacteria. Long-term antibiotics are usually adequate to treat a lung abscess, but sometimes the abscess must be drained by a <a href="/wiki/Surgery" title="Surgery">surgeon</a> or <a href="/wiki/Interventional_radiology" title="Interventional radiology">radiologist</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yu2011_133-8"><a href="#cite_note-Yu2011-133">[132]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Respiratory_and_circulatory_failure">Respiratory and circulatory failure</span></h3>
<p>Pneumonia can cause respiratory failure by triggering <a href="/wiki/Acute_respiratory_distress_syndrome" title="Acute respiratory distress syndrome">acute respiratory distress syndrome</a> (ARDS), which results from a combination of infection and inflammatory response. The lungs quickly fill with fluid and become stiff. This stiffness, combined with severe difficulties extracting oxygen due to the alveolar fluid, may require long periods of <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_ventilation" title="Mechanical ventilation">mechanical ventilation</a> for survival.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M31_39-9"><a href="#cite_note-M31-39">[38]</a></sup> Other causes of circulatory failure are <a href="/wiki/Hypoxemia" title="Hypoxemia">hypoxemia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Inflammation" title="Inflammation">inflammation</a>, and increased coagulability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elena_2015_36-19"><a href="#cite_note-Elena_2015-36">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sepsis" title="Sepsis">Sepsis</a> is a potential complication of pneumonia but usually occurs in people with poor immunity or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyposplenism" title="Hyposplenism">hyposplenism</a>. The organisms most commonly involved are <i>Streptococcus pneumoniae</i>, <i>Haemophilus influenzae</i>, and <i>Klebsiella pneumoniae</i>. Other causes of the symptoms should be considered such as a <a href="/wiki/Myocardial_infarction" title="Myocardial infarction">myocardial infarction</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_embolism" title="Pulmonary embolism">pulmonary embolism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-C250_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-C250-134">[133]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Epidemiology">Epidemiology</span></h2>

color:black;"> </span> no data</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffff65; color:black;"> </span> less than 100</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#fff200; color:black;"> </span> 100–700</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffdc00; color:black;"> </span> 700–1,400</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffc600; color:black;"> </span> 1,400–2,100</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffb000; color:black;"> </span> 2,100–2,800</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ff9a00; color:black;"> </span> 2,800–3,500</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ff8400; color:black;"> </span> 3,500–4,200</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ff6e00; color:black;"> </span> 4,200–4,900</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ff5800; color:black;"> </span> 4,900–5,600</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ff4200; color:black;"> </span> 5,600–6,300</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ff2c00; color:black;"> </span> 6,300–7,000</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#cb0000; color:white;"> </span> more than 7,000</div> </div></div></div></div>
<p>Pneumonia is a common illness affecting approximately 450 million people a year and occurring in all parts of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet11_12-20"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet11-12">[12]</a></sup> It is a major cause of death among all age groups resulting in 4 million deaths (7% of the world's total death) yearly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet11_12-21"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet11-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CochraneTx13_13-4"><a href="#cite_note-CochraneTx13-13">[13]</a></sup> Rates are greatest in children less than five, and adults older than 75 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet11_12-22"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet11-12">[12]</a></sup> It occurs about five times more frequently in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developing_world" title="Developing world">developing world</a> than in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developed_world" title="Developed world">developed world</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet11_12-23"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet11-12">[12]</a></sup> Viral pneumonia accounts for about 200 million cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet11_12-24"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet11-12">[12]</a></sup> In the United States, as of 2009, pneumonia is the 8th leading cause of death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clinic2011_25-17"><a href="#cite_note-Clinic2011-25">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Children">Children</span></h3>
<p>In 2008, pneumonia occurred in approximately 156 million children (151 million in the developing world and 5 million in the developed world).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet11_12-25"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet11-12">[12]</a></sup> In 2010, it resulted in 1.3 million deaths, or 18% of all deaths in those under five years, of which 95% occurred in the developing world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet11_12-26"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet11-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Develop11_24-10"><a href="#cite_note-Develop11-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[135]</a></sup> Countries with the greatest burden of disease include India (43 million), China (21 million) and Pakistan (10 million).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[136]</a></sup> It is the leading cause of death among children in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low_income_countries" title="Low income countries">low income countries</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet11_12-27"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet11-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CochraneTx13_13-5"><a href="#cite_note-CochraneTx13-13">[13]</a></sup> Many of these deaths occur in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neonatal" title="Neonatal">newborn</a> period. The <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> estimates that one in three newborn infant deaths is due to pneumonia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-garenne_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-garenne-138">[137]</a></sup> Approximately half of these deaths can be prevented, as they are caused by the bacteria for which an effective vaccine is available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[138]</a></sup> In 2011, pneumonia was the most common reason for admission to the hospital after an emergency department visit in the U.S. for infants and children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[139]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WPA_Pneumonia_Poster.jpg"><img alt='A poster with a shark in the middle of it, which reads "Pneumonia Strikes Like a Man-Eating Shark Led by its Pilot Fish the Common Cold"' class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="424" decoding="async" height="332" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/WPA_Pneumonia_Poster.jpg/220px-WPA_Pneumonia_Poster.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/WPA_Pneumonia_Poster.jpg/330px-WPA_Pneumonia_Poster.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/77/WPA_Pneumonia_Poster.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WPA_Pneumonia_Poster.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Works_Progress_Administration" title="Works Progress Administration">WPA</a> poster, 1936/1937</div></div></div>
<p>Pneumonia has been a common disease throughout human history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-History03_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-History03-141">[140]</a></sup> The word is from Greek πνεύμων (pneúmōn) meaning "lung".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[141]</a></sup> The symptoms were described by <a href="/wiki/Hippocrates" title="Hippocrates">Hippocrates</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 460</span>–370 BC):<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-History03_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-History03-141">[140]</a></sup> "Peripneumonia, and pleuritic affections, are to be thus observed: If the fever be acute, and if there be pains on either side, or in both, and if expiration be if cough be present, and the sputa expectorated be of a blond or livid color, or likewise thin, frothy, and florid, or having any other character different from the common... When pneumonia is at its height, the case is beyond remedy if he is not purged, and it is bad if he has dyspnoea, and urine that is thin and acrid, and if sweats come out about the neck and head, for such sweats are bad, as proceeding from the suffocation, rales, and the violence of the disease which is obtaining the upper hand."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hippo_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-hippo-143">[142]</a></sup> However, Hippocrates referred to pneumonia as a disease "named by the ancients". He also reported the results of surgical drainage of empyemas. <a href="/wiki/Maimonides" title="Maimonides">Maimonides</a> (1135–1204 AD) observed: "The basic symptoms that occur in pneumonia and that are never lacking are as follows: acute fever, sticking <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pleuritic" title="Pleuritic">pleuritic</a> pain in the side, short rapid breaths, serrated <a href="/wiki/Pulse" title="Pulse">pulse</a> and cough."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-maimo_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-maimo-144">[143]</a></sup> This clinical description is quite similar to those found in modern textbooks, and it reflected the extent of medical knowledge through the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> into the 19th century.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Edwin_Klebs" title="Edwin Klebs">Edwin Klebs</a> was the first to observe bacteria in the airways of persons having died of pneumonia in 1875.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-klebs_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-klebs-145">[144]</a></sup> Initial work identifying the two common bacterial causes, <i>Streptococcus pneumoniae</i> and <i>Klebsiella pneumoniae</i>, was performed by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Friedl%C3%A4nder" title="Carl Friedländer">Carl Friedländer</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fried_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-fried-146">[145]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Albert_Fraenkel_(1848%E2%80%931916)" title="Albert Fraenkel (1848–1916)">Albert Fraenkel</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fraenkel_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-fraenkel-147">[146]</a></sup> in 1882 and 1884, respectively. Friedländer's initial work introduced the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gram_staining" title="Gram staining">Gram stain</a>, a fundamental laboratory test still used today to identify and categorize bacteria. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_Gram" title="Christian Gram">Christian Gram</a>'s paper describing the procedure in 1884 helped to differentiate the two bacteria, and showed that pneumonia could be caused by more than one microorganism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gram_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-gram-148">[147]</a></sup> In 1887, Jaccond demonstrated pneumonia may be caused by opportunistic bacteria always present in the lung.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[148]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sir <a href="/wiki/William_Osler" title="William Osler">William Osler</a>, known as "the father of modern medicine", appreciated the death and disability caused by pneumonia, describing it as the "captain of the men of death" in 1918, as it had overtaken <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a> as one of the leading causes of death in this time. This phrase was originally coined by <a href="/wiki/John_Bunyan" title="John Bunyan">John Bunyan</a> in reference to "consumption" (tuberculosis).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[150]</a></sup> Osler also described pneumonia as "the old man's friend" as death was often quick and painless when there were much slower and more painful ways to die.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBMED05_22-9"><a href="#cite_note-EBMED05-22">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Viral pneumonia was first described by <a href="/wiki/Hobart_Reimann" title="Hobart Reimann">Hobart Reimann</a> in 1938. Reimann, Chairman of the Department of Medicine at <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson_University" title="Thomas Jefferson University">Jefferson Medical College</a>, had established the practice of routinely typing the pneumococcal organism in cases where pneumonia presented. Out of this work, the distinction between viral and bacterial strains was noticed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[151]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several developments in the 1900s improved the outcome for those with pneumonia. With the advent of <a href="/wiki/Penicillin" title="Penicillin">penicillin</a> and other antibiotics, modern surgical techniques, and intensive care in the 20th century, mortality from pneumonia, which had approached 30%, dropped precipitously in the developed world. Vaccination of infants against <i><a href="/wiki/Haemophilus_influenzae" title="Haemophilus influenzae">Haemophilus influenzae</a></i> type B began in 1988 and led to a dramatic decline in cases shortly thereafter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adams_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-adams-153">[152]</a></sup> Vaccination against <i>Streptococcus pneumoniae</i> in adults began in 1977, and in children in 2000, resulting in a similar decline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-whit_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-whit-154">[153]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society_and_culture">Society and culture</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_notable_pneumonia_cases" title="List of notable pneumonia cases">List of notable pneumonia cases</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Awareness">Awareness</span></h3>
<p>Due to the relatively low awareness of the disease, 12 November was declared as the annual <a href="/wiki/World_Pneumonia_Day" title="World Pneumonia Day">World Pneumonia Day</a>, a day for concerned citizens and policy makers to take action against the disease, in 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[155]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Costs">Costs</span></h3>
<p>The global economic cost of community-acquired pneumonia has been estimated at $17 billion annually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clinic2011_25-18"><a href="#cite_note-Clinic2011-25">[24]</a></sup> Other estimates are considerably higher. In 2012 the estimated aggregate costs of treating pneumonia in the United States were $20 billion;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[156]</a></sup> the median cost of a single pneumonia-related hospitalization is over $15,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[157]</a></sup> According to data released by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centers_for_Medicare_and_Medicaid_Services" title="Centers for Medicare and Medicaid Services">Centers for Medicare and Medicaid Services</a>, average 2012 hospital charges for inpatient treatment of uncomplicated pneumonia in the U.S. were $24,549 and ranged as high as $124,000. The average cost of an emergency room consult for pneumonia was $943 and the average cost for medication was $66.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[158]</a></sup> Aggregate annual costs of treating pneumonia in Europe have been estimated at €10 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[159]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note--BMJ2012-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref--BMJ2012_6-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFCaldeiraAlarcãoVaz-CarneiroCosta2012">Caldeira D, Alarcão J, Vaz-Carneiro A, Costa J (July 2012). <a class="external href="https://doi.org/10.1136%2Fbmj.e4260" rel="nofollow">10.1136/bmj.e4260</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMC_(identifier)" title="PMC (identifier)">PMC</a> <span class="cs1-lock-free"
